        "kids": "We all see the Sun every day but no person can visit it, so in 2018 NASA sent a brave robot called Parker to go super close and take a look. In this short video we are watching from behind Parker's big sun shield as it zoomed near the Sun, getting as close as about five Sun widths above the hot surface. Its cameras took pictures for seven hours and then the pictures were squished into about five seconds so everything looks fast. You can see the Sun's glowing outer layer like a bright halo and huge puffs of hot gas crashing into each other, while tiny stars drift far in the background. The Sun gives us light and warmth, but it also blows a wind of tiny particles that can push on the top of our air, make colorful auroras, cause power problems, and even harm satellites that help our phones and TV.",
        "original": "Everybody sees the Sun. Nobody's been there. Starting in 2018, though, NASA launched the robotic Parker Solar Probe (PSP) to investigate regions near to the Sun for the first time. The featured time-lapse video shows the view looking sideways from behind PSP's Sun shield in December during the closest approach of any human-made spacecraft to the Sun, looping down to only about five solar diameters above the Sun's hot surface. The PSP's Wide Field Imager for Solar Probe (WISPR) cameras took these images over seven hours, but they are digitally compressed here into about 5 seconds. The solar corona, including colliding coronal mass ejections (CMEs), is visible here in unprecedented detail, with stars passing far in the background. The Sun is not only Earth's dominant energy source, but its variable solar wind also compresses Earth's atmosphere, triggers auroras, affects power grids, and can even damage orbiting communication satellites.",
        "summarized": "NASA launched the Parker Solar Probe in 2018 to explore regions near the Sun for the first time. A time lapse from PSP's Wide Field Imager taken during its closest approach in December shows the view from behind the probe's sun shield down to about five solar diameters above the solar surface. Seven hours of images are compressed into about five seconds, revealing the corona and colliding coronal mass ejections in unprecedented detail with background stars visible. The Sun is Earths main energy source and its variable solar wind compresses the atmosphere, triggers auroras, disrupts power grids, and can damage orbiting communication satellites."
